B008B3H4VQ|2.0|I'm making an effort to be generous to this album (and its creators and fans). I should first concede that my musical tastes have graduated many a time since my high school days, when Hybrid Theory came along (in all its timely glory) to claim a lucrative, pinnacle seat atop a sub-genre that was already enjoying a fleeting climax, or honeymoon, if you prefer.  Bear with me here--this leads to Living Things, I promise. Linkin Park has always capitalized on the lowest common denominator, though that's not to say they haven't had true moments of brilliance. In many ways, it's a shame that Linkin Park catapulted into the limelight in the precarious way they did; not because they didn't deserve the attention, but because their name was immediately synonymous with a sub-genre, which came pre-loaded with an audience, acute expectations, and (as with all sub-genres) a death sentence. Artists whose work defines a (sub) genre for the fickle ears of the masses, seldom are able to do so again (i.e., beyond their original niche in the genre). What I'm getting at is that the best artist deals directly with Beauty, not with a specific vantage point on Beauty (the 'vantage point' being, in simple terms, the genre or sub-genre). For several albums, Linkin Park was locked in that same 'vantage point,' the same 'style' if you will, and IMO there are two main reasons this happens with so many bands: 1) It's all they know or care to do. Or, 2) They're letting their audience dictate their music. The larger the audience, the more oppressive the 'dictator.'  NOW, this all goes toward saying: holy sh*t did you notice what Linkin Park did with A Thousand Suns?! They reached their collective hand OUT of that box, albeit a slightly wavering hand, nevertheless, they did it! And of course, what happened is exactly what we (in the business of treating music as art) would expect: by far the lowest selling Linkin Park work to date and resounding admonishment from slews of idiot \\"fans\\" who can onlypraise what they'd expect, what's familiar, what's easy.  I was really hoping that Linkin Park would turn their backs on the stupid throng, in the name of something fresh and unexpected, in pursuit of that promise with which A Thousand Suns is ripe, in the name of art, by God! But no.  Living Things is an assured step BACK, a semi-stale platter of a dozen processed morsels catered to the Audience, punched out in tight 3-minute intervals in true assembly line fashion. Yes, in 2010 they reached their hands out of their fateful little box, only to have it slapped back by Mr. Audience, who stubbornly wants to consume, relive, and toss to the wayside--music as product.  Granted, this album's not ALL that bad. It's certainly worth $5, and it's probably worth 3 stars, but I hope that one day these guys will be able to say screw the audience and play from their bleeding hearts. I assure you, there are certainly nice little instances of brilliance on Living Things, but more often than this, I find myself sighing and at the very same moment think I detect a new, highly pitiable pain in Chester's voice.  It's a pain that says he's walking back and forth over bridges he could swear he remembers burning.

The book, however, could have been much shorter.  Contained within it are mini-biographies on MacArthur, Mao, Truman, and many of the other generals and major players - which is interesting, but again, not what I was expecting.<br /><br />In addition, and other reviewers have mentioned it, the author is definitely politically partisan.  He obviously has his own slant and much of the book read more like an opinion piece than a historical work.  He makes many judgment calls and even manages to bring in George W Bush and the author's opinion of Bush's handling of Iraq etc.<br /><br />All in all, it is, if nothing else, VERY thorough.  I would have liked far less opinion, far more accounts of the actual war and the battles as well as accounts from the soldiers (from reading this book, there were really only two or three battles in this war - Pusan, Chosin, and Chipyongni/Tunnels).  I came away with a much better understanding of the big picture of the war and the political environment of the time than I entered with.<br /><br />For anyone interested in history, it is essential that we know about the Korean War and this time in our nation's and the world's history.  It was the next chapter after WW2 and it preceded Vietnam and set the stage for much of our politics of even today.  I can't say I would recommend this book (if I did, it would be with great caution in terms of the author's opinions and slant) but I would recommend very much gaining an understanding of this war and this era.

Anastasia wants to know more about Christian than he wants to tell. He wants her to be open to his predilections, but she's reluctant. Isn't that dynamic common to all relationships, no matter how loving and non-sadomasochistic? While I often rolled my eyes at how the author tried to illustrate (or even just describe) this part of the evolving relationship between the two, their struggles rang true.

0385474547|4.0|Things Fall Apart, by Chinua Achebe, can really shake your moral foundation if you let it.  Written in the mid-twentieth century by a native Nigerian, Things Fall Apart is meant to depict the true culture of late nineteenth century West Africa.  In my opinion, Achebe succeeds in dispelling the myth that Africa was and is a cultureless, savage land full of senseless violence.  By telling the story of Okonkwo, his family, and their community, Achebe shows that though Nigerian culture may be very different from that of Western Europe or North America, it is no less a culture with structure and reason.<br />Chinua Achebe was born the son of a protestant Missionary in Nigeria in 1930.  Though he was educated primarily in English, he was fully immersed in the Ibo culture surrounding him.  Things Fall Apart is a novel he published in 1959 in response to widespread ignorance of African Culture.  The average western European or North American viewed Africa as cultureless and language-less, and Achebe decided that he may be able to change a few opinions.<br />Chinua Achebe's most important tool was language.  Achebe could have written in Ibo, but he chose to publish his novel in English. This was to be sure he reached his target audience, ignorant white men.  However, he chose very deliberately to use some Ibo words; words that cannot be translated precisely into English because of their complex nature.  These words include, among others, \\"nso-ani\\", describing a religious offense and \\"ajbala\\", referring to either a woman or to a man stripped of his titles.  He included a glossary of rough translations in the back of the novel, which I found to be instrumental in my understanding of the novel.<br />Another tool used by the author is emotional attachment to the main character, Okonkwo.  Okonkwo is a man's man with multiple wives living on his compound. From the perspective of a middle-class European, Okonkwo may be considered a savage. However, Achebe is sure to fully immerse his readers in the Ibo culture from the very beginning.  Okonkwo begins his journey as a respected man and I quickly became attached to him through an understanding of his cultural background.  The Ibo culture is patriarchal to the extreme. As a sheltered North American, it could have been a hard concept for me to appreciate, but being captivated by Achebe's writings I could not help but be swept into the Ibo culture right alongside Okonkwo.<br />I would categorize Things Fall Apart as a work of historical fiction.  The novel is very political and not an easy read.  But I felt at its conclusion that it was worth my time.  Things Fall Apart gives perspective that is otherwise lacking in my education thus far.  Anybody remotely interested in civil rights or in unfamiliar cultures would do well to give it a chance.<br />

The title acknowledges the bands thirtieth anniversary, and is this lineups third album of original material since they reformed in 2006.<br /><br />Their last album, \\"Omega,\\" was tremendous and somehow the band has managed to outdo themselves with \\"XXX.\\" Fans of Asia have come to expect catchy songs, but this collection really exceeds all expectations.<br /><br />What is unusual about \\"XXX,\\" is that the truly great songs are on the second half of the album. I say that as a fan who saw this band on their first US tour and also as the owner of the entire Wetton era catalog.<br /><br />One other thing that seems different is the tandem of Wetton and Palmer on bass and drums. If you enjoyed the band's debut thirty years ago you will appreciate this collection of songs. 1846613299|5.0|BOOK REVIEW<br /><br />APPROPRIATE DISPUTE RESOLUTION (ADR) IN IRELAND<br />A Handbook for Family Lawyers and their Clients<br /><br />By Josepha Madigan<br />Plus a team of specialist contributors<br /><br />Family Law - Jordan Publishing Ltd<br /><br />ISBN:  978 184661 329 6<br /><br />[...]<br /><br />ADR BY ANOTHER NAME: A POSITIVE AND PRACTICAL APPROACH OF INTEREST TO UK PRACTITIONERS AS WELL AS THOSE IN IRELAND -<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />What's in a name?  In England and Wales, `ADR' means `Alternative Dispute Resolution'; in Ireland ADR stands for Appropriate Dispute Resolution. (In Australia, the same process is known as External Dispute Resolution.) So is there a difference?  Does the difference in nomenclature indicate a difference in attitude or orientation, or ethos?<br /><br />This rather absorbing book by Josepha Madigan, with its clear and practical approach, enables lawyers and other professionals in both jurisdictions to compare the UK system - if that's the right word - with its Irish counterpart and vice versa.<br /><br />Note that the expert author is a member of the Law Society of England and Wales as well as the Law Society of Ireland.<br /><br />In addition to a lengthy list of qualifications in this field, she is a Resolution trained and accredited mediator, specialising in family disputes and separating clients.<br /><br />It seems that in Ireland, barristers and solicitors have a duty to provide information and advice on mediation, following the draft Mediation Bill 2012  This of course indicates a stronger stance on mediation than that which currently prevails in England and Wales, where practitioners are encouraged by many judges -- but not all -- to mediate, or seek a collaborative solution before going to court.<br /><br />The point is made in the foreword to the book that in Ireland (as elsewhere) family law issues are becoming so much more complex, with an ever-expanding range of issues and participants, such as single parents, same sex couples, sperm donors, foreign partners (whose cultural differences need to be recognised) and so on.<br /><br />The adversarial system in many of these cases is herein described as a `blunt instrument' for resolving such disputes.  The remark is made that `all initiatives (including mediation) designed to improve the resolution of family law disputes are to be welcomed'.<br /><br />The publication of this book is therefore timely, it being, apparently, the first title on this topic devoted to requirements of Irish professionals, as well as their clients who can also benefit from the practical guidance provided on ADR.  Certainly, the author explores the pitfalls of ADR as well as its advantages.<br /><br />The book is designed, she states `to empower clients with a viable and often more appropriate mechanism for resolving family law disputes' in the hope that the book will emerge as `a reference tool for the judiciary, lawyers, mediators, collaborative practitioners, counsellors psychologists, psychiatrists, therapists, GPs and all those who are at the coalface of...relationship breakdown.'<br /><br />For all those who find themselves at this selfsame `coalface', this book should rank as a must-read work of reference and an invaluable acquisition for any professional involved in this complex and sensitive area. Reference tools abound, including tables of cases, statutes and statutory instruments, as well as a very useful table of European and International Legislation.  The publication date is cited as at May 2012.h an ever-expanding range of issues and participants, such as single parents, same sex couples, sperm donors, foreign partners (whose cultural differences need to be recognised) and so on.<br /><br />The adversarial system in many of these cases is herein described as a `blunt instrument' for resolving such disputes. B004SBX112|3.0|For those of you complaining about the app not letting you update unless you're on wifi, you're wrongly sabotaging the ratings for this product. B001992NW4|5.0|Hmmm, my oh my, \\"The Exorcist\\" film version is a strange one. First off, and it might be trivial at this point to mention it, the film version is very loosely based upon the novel itself. Friedkin's film is an agnostic treatment of this subject matter, not a truly belief-based interpretation. Even though the director put back in some of the missing \\"carpentry\\" (as Blatty refers to) in the \\"Version You've Never Seen\\", there are still a great deal of unexplained questions. This movie is not really about the occult all that much; it is more of a family-in-crisis kind of drama, with a lot of religious overtones tossed in. The novel includes scenes that make the threat of the supernatural more understandable to the reader (in the film, Chris has no book on occult lore laying around the house, the medium who showed up at her party warning her to take the \\"toy\\" away from Regan, the sub-plot about Karl's daughter, etc. have all been stripped away for this abbreviated film interpretation). In the film, things happen in far too abrupt fashion, with scenes of blasphemy mixed in with scenes of spiritual faith, none of which ever gels completely. Also, as one reviewer on here stated before, Blatty has not really done his best to tell a cohesive story. It appears that there is a battle between good and evil going on, but the old Jesuit priest's conflict with the demon is perhaps one of the most mis-understood pyschological Western-type motifs ever committed to celluloid (everybody keeps referring to the entity as \\"the Devil,\\" or \\"Satan\\", not the real character shown in statue form in the movie). Friedkin's film is far more empathetic to the plight of Chris, and this is no doubt a strong feminist movie (despite poor Linda's abusive scenes) in the director's hands. Therefore, we end up with a movie that is entirely humanist, but no less fascinating (and superbly entertaining, in my opinion, at least). This is not a detective movie, either, despite the brilliant Lee J. Cobb's thankless role. Finally, as for this being a horror film, I ultimately have to disagree with this conclusion...there is nothing here that has the INTENTION to frighten viewers as much as it does to make a viewers THINK, and therefore it is more dramatic (I admit quite intense at times) than scary. One reason this film will stand the test of time (so to speak) and keep fascinating viewers today who might be new to it is simply for the reason that, unlike most of today's scare pictures, this one is about age old ideas concerning themes that we all have some steak in (life and death, c'mon) and a set of terrific performances from every actor involved, despite the thin storyline that they were given to work with, they really bring these performances home in a unique fashion (Miller is intensely good here, but so are Von Sydow, Burstyn, sweet Linda, etc.) Anyway, this movie also has some funny moments that I believe to be intentional (the phone ringing while Karras is listening to the tape comes to mind).<br />As for this BluRay presentation, one could not ask for better. Every extra that any sane person could wish for is here, and the picture and sound are superlative. Earth-shakingly good effects, sound design, etc. After \\"The Exorcist\\", I think the horror film underwent a dramatic revision in terms of the use of sound effects and music, as the old \\"jump out of your seat\\" music that informed you that a scene would be terrifying (like in \\"Psycho\\") would no longer be so obviously, or so routinely, used ever again. I enjoy the expanded cut more so because to me it is, in a sense, the most complete version (despite some silly effects shots that really shouldn't have been added). Bachman--unlike King--is a bitter, unpleasant author that really has no love for his reader. That's okay, because it is sort of an act. B000003N7K|5.0|I was lucky enough to interview Mrs. Tedi Hartman (Johnny's widow) for [[ASIN:0810882817 The Last Balladeer: The Johnny Hartman Story (Studies in Jazz)]]. When I mentioned his recordings were often used as a mood-setter she shared how Johnny once said that if he could have earned a royalty everytime a man used one of his records to get lucky with a lady, he would have made more money than he did from record sales.<br /><br />These exceptional musicans came together for a three-hour session and made this timeless album of romantic ballads. Unlike is often reported, the tracks were not all \\"first takes\\" but they approach a level of perfection hard to fathom considering the songs were captured in an era when recordings consisted of complete live takes with no autotune post-production. Except for some extra reverb, what you hear on the album is just what was recorded live to tape.<br /><br />Coltrane and Hartman had not played together before preparing for this session and they never saw each other again (Coltrane passed away only four years later). But for one afternoon they fused together a beautiful artistic creation. Some have lamented the brevity of the album but I choose to celebrate the fact that during the thirty-two minutes it takes to hear these six tracks, any desparate,clinging hope for romance is possible, and with any luck at all, I will owe Johnny a royalty.<br />~Gregg Akkerman

B004SIP9E2|5.0|It isn't often that a movie mines a vein of obvious jokes and makes them hilarious. \\"Scary Movie's charm is that it mines the horror genre - not usually a place for laughs, and relentlessly pokes fun at the genre's absurdities and silly, stock characters. There's the school beauty, played by Carmen Electra, who finds herself pursued by a knife wielding maniac while home alone at night. Of course, the house is made almost completely of glass, making her ludicrously visible to the attacker lurking outside. When she escapes the killer, she encounters two signs pointing in opposite directions -- \\"Safety\\" and \\"Death.\\" You don't have to be Houdini to know which direction she picks.<br /><br />But there's lots more! The film takes another of the genre's tropes - the virgin couple desperate to get it on - and runs with it. There is no way to discuss the hilarity, but it involves an electrified chastity belt, barbed wire, safety goggles, a chain saw and a geyser of...well, watch the film! There are jokes on pot-smoking, breast implants, gay panic, guilt over past misdeeds, the Matrix and much more. Not being a big horror fan, I was worried that I wouldn't get the references to horror films. But I needn't have worried. The film is terrifically funny, even if you are only vaguely familiar with the genre's standard themes. For a \\"dumb\\" movie, the acting was first rate. Kudos especially to Anna Faris as Cindy Campbell, the cute virgin girl, who has a intelligently comic face and great timing.<br /><br />\\"Scary Movie,\\" the first installment in what has become an industry, has the purity and the fearlessness that comes from filmmakers just going for it. Not knowing whether there would be sequels, they make a film that is as funny as it can be made. A fabulous effort, with many memorable moments, even if my wife wouldn't join me to watch it!

B004A5EWZK|3.0|These episodes are complete, containing scenes not shown on BBC America. Historical consequences continue to ripple from the hefty rocks launched by King Henry VIII, affecting the world today. The Tudor period is interesting, even without embellishment. \\"The Tudors\\" indulges in mixing fascinating truth with historical fiction, so you may enjoy verifying the facts after viewing. The sets and costumes are visually rich, a nice escape from contemporary sparseness.<br /><br />The U.K. abounds with burly redheaded men (and actors) whose visages perfectly mimic that of King Henry VIII, they're as common as the hearty meat pies they resemble. We're quite familiar with what Henry VIII actually looked like. Poor little Jonathan Rhys Myers would be lost inside King Henry VIII's armor; what woeful miscasting. And in the opening scene with Henry administering pressure to his personal needs, Showtime shlock is demanded of Meyers and inflicted on viewers. The obligatory multitude of heaving bosoms is repetitive; the fine actors and audience deserve better. In the last Season, young Myers is crudely \\"aged.\\" This isn't the actor's fault, but the director's, whose idea of an older Henry VIII has Meyers jutting his chin like an ersatz Orson Welles, grumbling, \\"You shall have no wine before it's time.\\" Sadly, Meyers subsequently has been plagued by excesses of alcohol. Sam Waddington, the excellent actor who portrays Edward Stafford (an ancestor of my spouse, so I'm biased), the 3rd Duke of Buckingham, would have been a much more appropriate choice, though perhaps he wouldn't have submitted to Showtime's Henry-sleazy. Waddington is from the same area once dominated by the Plantagenet House of York. Unlike the story in \\"The Tudors,\\" the first conflict between the 3rd Duke of Buckingham and King Henry VIII regarded Henry's seduction of the Duke's sister, Anne Stafford, a conflict not explored in the series.<br /><br />The tragic Anne Boleyn, mother of Queen Elizabeth I, is masterfully played by Natalie Dormer, who shines in the role. There are stories behind stories with \\"The Tudors.\\" Unfortunately, the creators of this series weren't particularly interested in depicting accurate events. Lady Margaret Pole (poorly depicted in \\"The Tudors\\") was persecuted by Henry VIII, her son in-law, the 1st Barron Stafford, was the son of the executed Edward Stafford, the 3rd Duke of Buckingham (Waddington). Stafford's mother was the daughter of the 4th Earl of Northumberland. Her nephew, Henry Percy, was said to have had an unfortunate secret marriage to Anne Boleyn. Later, he was forced to marry another woman, and was present at Anne's \\"trial,\\" after which he collapsed; dying a few months later.  Henry's hapless fifth wife, Katherine Howard (well-played by Tazmin Merchant), was Anne's first-cousin, the niece of Anne's mother (Katherine's father and Anne's mother were siblings). Henry couldn't leave the Howards alone. The depiction of the Dowager Duchess of Norfolk, Katherine's step-grandmother, who raised her, is oddly scewed.<br /><br />The worse Henry VIII's chance of providing legitimate male successors became, the more he pruned branches of the Plantagenet tree with more authentic claims to the throne than himself. Countess Salisbury, Lady Margaret Pole, was a lady-in-waiting to Catherine of Aragon, and governess to Princess Mary. Margaret's family was viciously persecuted by Henry VIII because they had many direct lines to the throne. Later, her son, Cardinal Reginald Pole, was the last Catholic Archbishop of Canterbury. Her daughter, Ursula (1504-1570), married Henry Stafford, the 1st Barron Stafford. His father (played by Sam Waddington) Edward Stafford, the 3rd Duke of Buckingham, is depicted with historical inaccuracies in the first episodes of \\"The Tudors.\\" Buckingham was the great-great grandson of Edward the III. He had royal blood through Thomas of Woodstock, Duke of Gloucester, Earl of Buckingham, younger son of Edward III, and brother of John ofGaunt; his mother, Catherine Woodville, was sister to Edward IV's queen, the lovely Elizabeth Woodville. These two sisters were descended from the de Luxembourg rulers of the Holy Roman Empire, and noble Orsini and de Balzo families.<br /><br />Lady Salisbury (Pole) was the eldest daughter of the George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence, and the former Isabella Neville, elder daughter of Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick, and Anne Beauchamp, his wife, who inherited the Earldom of Warwick. George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence, 1st Earl of Salisbury, 1st Earl of Warwick, (1449 - 1478) was the third son of Richard Plantagenet, 3rd Duke of York, and Cecily Neville. He was the brother of King Edward IV and King Richard III. His wife, Isabella, was the sister of Queen Anne, wife of King Richard III. George's mother, Cecily Neville, Duchess of York, was the daughter of Sir Ralph Neville, 1st Earl of Westmorland, and Joan Beaufort, daughter of John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster, a Plantagenet, the 3rd surviving son of Edward the III, and father of Henry IV. These families were all interwoven.<br /><br />The arrest and brutal execution (considered grotesque even by that society's bloodthirsty standards) of Lady Margaret Pole (1473-1541) in \\"The Tudors\\" depicts her as cowardly. The historical record indicates her brave defiance and devout faith. For some reason, the show's writers transferred her execution onto Thomas Cromwell, perhaps to make him appear the larger martyr. Lady Salisbury was married to Sir Richard Pole (his mother, Edith St. John, was the half sister of King Henry VII's mother, Lady Margaret Beaufort). Her grandfather was Richard Neville, 16th Earl of Warwick, the \\"Kingmaker,\\" the wealthiest and most powerful English peer of his age, with international political connections, a main protagonist in the Wars of the Roses. The elder of his two daughters, Isabel, married George Plantagenet, Duke of Clarence (daughter, Margaret Pole). Neville's younger daughter, Queen Anne, after a short-lived marriage to King Henry VI's son Edward of Westminster (who died young), married King Edward's younger brother Richard, Duke of Gloucester, who later became King Richard III. Anne was originally betrothed to Richard; he rescued her from captivity as George's ward. George wanted her massive inheritance in addition to her sister's.<br /><br />The depiction of the Catholic uprising, the [[ASIN:1842126660 Pilgrimage of Grace: The Rebellion That Shook Henry VIII's Throne]], has a bare minimum of detail; the Percy, Neville, and even Stafford families were deeply involved. This was a conflict between relatives about religious beliefs and power, whose outcome was fundamental to England; it would define who sat on the throne, a Tudor, or a Yorkist Plantagenet. Henry VIII, for all his foibles, sat triumphant in his sphere of assumed spiritual and political authority, largely due to all the family in-fighting  dating back to [[ASIN:B004XC5LQE The Wars of the Roses: A Bloody Crown]].Gaunt; his mother, Catherine Woodville, was sister to Edward IV's queen, the lovely Elizabeth Woodville. 0307730697|5.0|I have heard about the book Fearless for a few months now, and even posted earlier that I had read the first chapter of the book, so I was very excited to find that WaterBrook Press was offering the book for review.  I read this book in two sittings, which really would have been one sitting if I didn't plan to get ready for a family reunion at my home over the weekend.  This book is just so good that it was very hard to put it down.  And like really great books, I did go through a few tissues, especially at the end.  I spent about twenty minutes during a drive with my teenagers yesterday excitedly re-telling stories from the book (with them listening wide-eyed).<br /><br />Fearless, written by Eric Blehm, tells the life story of Adam Brown, a man who should go down in history as one of our American heroes. The book tells the good, the bad and the ugly in Adam's life, including a stint with drugs and a stay in prison.  It tells the incredible story of how Adam overcame physical wounds that would have sidelined any other man (including Navy Seals) to advance to the top level, DEVGRU (Naval Special Warfare Development Group), upon which time all records are labeled \\"Top Secret\\".<br /><br />Even though I knew the end of the story, it was still very hard to accept the fact that Adam gave his life for his country.  I wanted him to live on forever.  But as author Eric Blehm points out Adam was a Christian, a man who professed Christ as his Savior, and his life and his witness did not end with his death.<br /><br />Adam's witness at home and in the field affected many people, from the Afghan children he took shoes to during the cold months (over 500 pairs were distributed by Adam, collected from his family, fellow Seals and churches), to the way he engaged in battle.  He helped his wife teach Sunday school.  He brought fellow Seals to church with him.  One Seal struggled with the concept of Christianity, but accepted Christ shortly before he was killed on a mission.  Many lives were changed because of Adam's faith and example.<br /><br />The author makes a statement in his Afterword that speaks volumes to the character of Adam Brown:<br />And though I hadn't opened a Bible in more than twenty-five years, his (Adam Brown's) faith encouraged me to question my own questioning about religion.<br /><br />This is a book you might want to reserve for older, mature teenagers and adults.  Those who can handle the graphic nature of the book, as well as a few choice words sprinkled in, mainly in the beginning of the book.  Overall, this is a book that I will be sharing with my sons, a couple of my nephews and other family members.  I believe it is important that we choose heroes who actually do what is right, those who serve our country and try to make our lives better, and not those who are portrayed as \\"heroes\\" today on television shows.<br /><br />In order to comply with new Federal Trade Commission regulations, please note that this book was provided compliments of  WaterBrook Press.

0307887448|3.0|I wanted to like this book, and for a while I actually loved.  But despite a bunch of cool ideas, \\"Ready Player One\\" doesn't seem to know what kind of book it wants to be, or what audience it's trying to reach.<br /><br />In the not-too-distant future of \\"Ready Player One\\", unemployment is soaring, millions of Americans work in indentured servitude to pay their debts, and natural resources are being depleted at a dizzying rate.  To escape the problems of real life, most people spend as much time as possible within the virtual reality world of the Oasis.  As the novel opens, the game's creator dies and announces in a video will that he has hidden a quest within the game, and the first to complete it will inherit his entire fortune.  This of course sets everyman players like orphaned teenager Wade into furious--and deadly--competition with a large (and of course evil) corporation.<br /><br />What the novel does well, it does extremely well: in the world of \\"Ready Player One\\", urban sprawl has resulted in an epidemic shortage of housing, the remedy to which has been to erect skyscraper trailer parks: beaten down double-wides stacked on top of each other, reaching hundreds of feet into the sky, swaying dangerously with the wind and very regularly collapsing; it's an image hauntingly rendered, and is a refreshingly original addition to the tropes of dystopian fiction.  \\"Ready Player One\\" has several nice details like this, all making the novel's world engaging and believable, and the characters who inhabit it sympathetic.<br /><br />But at around the midway point, it all starts to fall apart.  The novel's best quality--it's depiction of a bleak American future--is pushed to the background in favor of its worst aspects.  The creator of the Oasis was obsessed with the 1980s, and so the world of the game (and, in turn, the novel) is littered with over-detailed representations of pop culture from that decade.  At first this is kind of fun for the reader--discussions of forgotten classic arcade games and science fiction movies are fun for fans of the time period--but it gets tired, around the time several pages are dedicated to a detailed (and I mean very detailed) description of the '80s movie \\"Wargames\\".  And it doesn't get any more enjoyable when, later on, \\"Monty Python and the Holy Grail\\" is given the same treatment.  The cumulative effect of these and other '80s references is to make the reader say, \\"Great, Mr. author, you know who holds the world's high score on Donkey Kong.  So what?\\"  To be blunt, the novel gets boring, right around the time it should be at its most compelling.<br /><br />There are myriad other problems, as well: characters have a habit of suddenly sounding like television PSAs when they regularly begin preaching about environmental concerns.  It's a message that I philosophically agree with, but I don't need to hear about global warming and oil over-consumption in my video game novels.  And a love story between protagonist Wade and a rival player that would be more at home in a YA book consumes the whole plot by the novel's conclusion.<br /><br />To run the risk of sounding flippant, for those readers who are obsessed with both online gaming and '80s pop culture, this may be the best book you read all year.  For everyone else, it's more of an experience of \\"what could have been\\".  If nothing else, I don't think I've ever read a book set primarily within a video game, so there's that.

0807014273|5.0|Viktor Frankl needs no introductions. I first read \\"Man's Search for Meaning\\" (\\"'''' '''' ''''''\\") during my military service and I recall it making a great impression on me. Reading it now, more than twenty years later, it is still a remarkably moving and impressive book (especially given how short it is).<br /><br />In concise and somewhat dry prose, without resorting to over dramatisation, Frankl describes his personal experiences as a Holocaust survivor. He asks a basic question: how can the concentration camp experience help explain why people hang on to life. Frankl uses the experiences from the concentration camps, perhaps the worst possible place for a human being to believe and hang on to life, in order to formulate a psychotherapeutic method in finding a reason to live (Logotherapy). He describes the three stages a typical concentration camp prisoner goes through - shock, apathy and disillusionment - and concludes that regardless of the hardships every person finds a reason to live. For one person it might be God or religion; for another it might be a beloved one waiting for him on the outside; for yet another it might be some personal goal that still needs to be accomplished. Whatever the reason, humans find a way to hang on to life in the worst possible circumstances.<br /><br />Frankl's basic conclusion is that basic tenement of Jewish faith: freedom of choice (although Frankl did not frame his theory in any religious terms). Ultimately, regardless of the conditions of life, freedom of choice remains the one determining factor of how a person will live. And Frankl says that the right choice to make in order to have a meaningful life is to help others find their meaning of life, to be a decent person, a mentsch. His examples of decent \\"good\\" Nazis and indecent \\"bad\\" prisoners illustrate how this personal choice can make all the difference.<br /><br />It is no wonder \\"Man's Search for Meaning\\" is considered one of the most influential books on the 20th century.ntury.

0812979702|5.0|This is a quite unique and valuable book, that emphasizes the mental game.  It is unique in that the author is not a highly skilled golfer.  His real forte is tennis, and his book on the inner game of tennis is what lead him to venture to the sport of golf.  To summarize his approach, he emphasizes getting away from relying mainly on a myriad of mechanical thoughts, and instead on learning to trust your native abilities.  He emphasizes things like relaxation techniques, ways of minimizing the excessive tension that afflicts all but the very finest players.  (Although even the very best sometimes focus on the mechanical so much that excessive tension creeps into their games.  A few years ago Justin Leonard was in the midst of a slump until he finally realized that his grip pressure had increased FOUR fold!)  I will say that the first edition of this book went to extremes, practically claiming that no mechanical thoughts at all were needed. His approach is a needed counterpoint to the excessively mechanical approach that the typical serious amateur  player takes.  'Paralysis by analysis' is a real problem, and by addressing mental side of the game, and learning to TRUST the innate abilities our bodies have rather than trying to make everything happen, you can free up your swing and play with more confidence and less frustration. The musical arrrangements on the track on the song \\"Anything We Want\\". The percussion instruments are beautifully arranged in this song. This song is about living with a sense of complete freedom. \\"Jonathan\\" is the first love song I have heard dedicated to a man.  This song is about how actions speak louder than words in a romantic relationship.  \\"Hot Knife\\" is another excellent song. I have never heard of a woman referred to as a pat of butter in a song. I like Fiona's creative lyrics in this song very much. \\"Left Alone\\" is a song with groovy piano playing and equally excellent drum playing.  This song is a downer lyrically, but the instruments make it special.  \\"Periphery\\" is a song that reminds me to appreciate the things I have.  I don't have to be envious of the things that other people possess. I love the music of Fiona Apple.

B0000524CY|5.0|I remember being assigned to read the book \\"The Exorcist\\" by William Peter Blatty, for a class entitled \\"Atheism and Theism\\" in the Theology curriculum at Marquette University when I was a student there in 1973. It was a night in late November when I sat down in an old rocking chair by the window in my room in the old boarding house I lived in that was mainly populated by Upper-level Marquette students. It was about 5:30 PM or so on a Thursday night, and I thought I would read this book for a few hours, which looked SO UNINTERESTING at the time.<br /><br />I had no inkling of the literary journey I was about to embark on.<br /><br />Snow started falling outside and shone heavily under the streetlight in the alley behind the house. It was a smooth feeling. It fell to a depth of at least 3-4 feet.<br /><br />Meanwhile, I was engrossed in one of the most fascinating books I have ever read. I didn't put it down until I was done...... around 6 AM the next morning. \\"The Exorcist\\" by William Peter Blatty remains one of my favorite books of all time.<br /><br />I saw the movie a short time later. It was ... and IS ... tremendous and terrifying. When Linda Blair's head did the 360 for the priests, there was wretching in the theatre. I had NEVER seen that before...and considering the time frame (almost 40 years ago) it was WAY AHEAD of its' time in imaginative terror. AND they, the book/movie, were supposedly based on a TRUE story.<br /><br />Ellen Burnstyn, Jason Miller, Linda Blair, Lee J. Cobb and Max Von Sydow as \\"the Exorcist\\" gave masterful performances. I especially liked Jason Miller's portrayal of the priest/physician/psychiatrist who ultimately tips the scale towards Good and God, and away from Evil and the devil, by giving his own life.<br /><br />A complex movie which almost certainly has to be watched a few times to get a feel for all that is put into this cinematic masterpiece. I enjoy watching it STILL, to this very day.

1451648537|5.0|This was an amazing biography, probably the best I have read.  It was packed full of information about Jobs that I didn't know, as well as about the computer industry as a whole.  He had, in my opinion, just the right amount of technical information to make you able to understand what Apple and other computer companies were doing, but not enough to bog down a lay person (in my opinion, anyway).  I actually found the tech history embedded in the book fascinating.<br /><br />What took this book from \\"good\\" or \\"great\\" to excellent was the last few chapters; the author clearly spent a great deal of time with Jobs before his death, and he really brought the emotion from Jobs' last months through to the reader in a way that not many biographers have the opportunity to do.  I really felt that I got to see an intimate view of Jobs', both at home and at work.  I was also amazed by the number of people he seems to have consulted in writing the book, and his ability to get a well rounded look at Jobs.<br /><br />On the other hand, I do think he got a little too wrapped up in the mythology of Jobs'; while he does a good job stating other people's negative views, and clearly showing Jobs' faults, I sometimes felt he was under-emphasizing or glossing over the fact that Jobs' inventions were often just improvements on other people's ideas.  He does tell us this, and he does at times discuss past attempts at the same idea, but it easily gets lost in his glowing description of how the products \\"revolutionized\\" technology.  While I would not want to understate the importance of the products Apple has produced, I think it's important to remember that very little of it was truly original (although,  certainly, they vastly improved on older versions), and I think that gets lots in the telling here at times.<br /><br />I also really do wish that a short Epilogue had been added after Jobs died; I know the publishing came very close on the heels of his death, so I am assuming it was just not feasible at the time, but I definitely felt like it detracted from the book that there was not at least a statement regarding when he died etc.  I had to go to Wikipedia to remind myself when I finished the book, and there was some great info. there about speeches and memorials etc. that would have made a great finisher to this book....even if it was only a couple of pages long.  Hopefully this will be added in a later edition.<br /><br />Reading the one star reviews, it seems like a lot of people don't exactly understand what a biography is; a lot of people seem to have read this book hoping for a psychological analysis of Jobs and/or an explanation of his approach to business.  That is NOT what a biography is.  A biography, as defined by dictionary.com is \\"a written account of another person's life\\"; i.e. a history.  If you approached this thinking that it would have Jobs' explanation on how to run a business, you were bound to be disappointed; and I for one would have been disappointed if that was what the book had turned out to be, I actually wanted a biography.  Not to mention, if you couldn't make SOME leaps yourself in figuring out why Steve was the way he was from the information presented here, I don't think the author can help you.  It's alright for an author to expect some analysis on your part.  This book did exactly what biographies do; it chronicled the life of the PERSON (not the company) that was the subject.<br /><br />Again, this was one of the best biographies I have read (and it prompted me to buy Issacson's Franklin biography), and I highly recommend it to anyone, regardless of whether they love or hate Jobs/Apple.  In fact, I think this book helps shed some light on WHY so many people dislike Jobs; it might not excuse the faults, but it does at least explain them.  Definitely one of my top 10 reads this year.ot feasible at the time, but I definitely felt like it detracted from the book that there was not at least a statement regarding when he died etc.  I had to go to Wikipedia to remind myself when I finished the book, and there was some great info. there about speeches and memorials etc. that would have made a great finisher to this book....even if it was only a couple of pages long. B000JMKO3C|4.0|Despite its rather bleak title, Veronika Decides to Die, is actually an exploration of what it means to be alive and to live an honest life. It explores the experience of a young woman, who, having survived a suicide attempt, finds herself in a mental asylum. Freed from the pressures of being sane, she is able to explore who she is and how she longs to live her life. I've avoided this book for a long time, largely because of the title, but it's actually a beautiful book, and one of Coelho's best. There's a messy bit at the beginning where Coelho awkwardly inserts himself in the story, tying his own much publicised time in mental institutions to the story, but once you get beyond that it's an inspiring read about leading an authentic life.
